Europort">Rosslare</a>\xe2\x80\x93Cherbourg</li>\n<li>Rosslare\xe2\x80\x93Roscoff</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Weymouth,_Dorset" title="Weymouth, Dorset">Weymouth</a>\xe2\x80\x93Saint Malo</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Channel_Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Channel Tunnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Channel_Tunnel" title="Channel Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</a></div>\n<p>Many travellers cross beneath the Channel using the Channel Tunnel, first proposed in the early 19th century and finally opened in 1994, connecting the UK and France by rail. It is now routine to travel between Paris or <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> and London on the <a href="/wiki/Eurostar" title="Eurostar">Eurostar</a> train. Freight trains also use the tunnel. Cars, coaches and lorries are carried on <a href="/wiki/Eurotunnel_Shuttle" title="Eurotunnel Shuttle">Eurotunnel Shuttle</a> trains between <a href="/wiki/Folkestone" title="Folkestone">Folkestone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_Mont_Saint-Michel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Le_Mont_Saint-Michel.jpg/220px-Le_Mont_Saint-Michel.jpg" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Le_Mont_Saint-Michel.jpg/330px-Le_Mont_Saint-Michel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Le_Mont_Saint-Michel.jpg/440px-Le_Mont_Saint-Michel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4666" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Le_Mont_Saint-Michel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mont_Saint-Michel" class="mw-redirect" title="Mont Saint-Michel">Mont Saint-Michel</a> is one of the most visited and recognisable landmarks on the English Channel.</div></div></div>\n<p>The coastal resorts of the Channel, such as <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">Brighton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deauville" title="Deauville">Deauville</a>, inaugurated an era of aristocratic tourism in the early 19th century, which developed into the seaside tourism that has shaped resorts around the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Short trips across the Channel for leisure purposes are often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Booze_cruise" title="Booze cruise">Channel Hopping</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Channel_crossings">History of Channel crossings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: History of Channel crossings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(January 2013)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>As one of the narrowest and most well-known international waterways lacking dangerous currents, the Channel has been the first objective of numerous innovative sea, air, and <a href="/wiki/Human_power" title="Human power">human powered</a> crossing technologies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\nPre-historic people sailed from the mainland to England for millennia. At the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last Ice Age</a>, lower sea levels even permitted <a href="/wiki/Doggerland" title="Doggerland">walking across</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-usask_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usask-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_boat">By boat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: By boat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table Many waterskiers have made this return crossing non-stop since this time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</td>\n<td>It took 12 hours 26 minutes 39 seconds and set a new Guinness World Record.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Pierre Andriel crossed the English Channel aboard the <i><a href="/wiki/Steam_ship_%C3%89lise" title="Steam ship \xc3\x89lise">\xc3\x89lise</a></i>, ex the Scottish p.s. "Margery" in March 1816, one of the earliest seagoing voyages by <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steam ship</a>.\n</p><p>The paddle steamer <i>Defiance</i>, Captain William Wager, was the first steamer to cross the Channel to Holland, arriving there on 9 May 1816.<sup id="cite_ref-Mariner_Mirror_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mariner_Mirror-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 10 June 1821, English-built <a href="/wiki/Paddle_steamer" title="Paddle steamer">paddle steamer</a> <i>Rob Roy</i> was the first passenger ferry to cross channel. The steamer was purchased subsequently by the French postal administration and renamed <i>Henri IV</i> and put into regular passenger service a year later. It was able to make the journey across the Straits of Dover in around three hours.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 1843, because of difficulties with Dover harbour, the South Eastern Railway company developed the <a href="/wiki/Boulogne-sur-Mer" title="Boulogne-sur-Mer">Boulogne-sur-Mer</a>-<a href="/wiki/Folkestone" title="Folkestone">Folkestone</a> route as an alternative to Calais-Dover. The first ferry crossed under the command of <a href="/wiki/Captain_Hayward" title="Captain Hayward">Captain Hayward</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1974 a Welsh coracle piloted by Bernard Thomas of Llechryd crossed the English Channel to France in 13\xc2\xbd hours. The journey was undertaken to demonstrate how the <a href="/wiki/Bull_Boat" class="mw-redirect" title="Bull Boat">Bull Boats</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Mandan" title="Mandan">Mandan</a> Indians of <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a> could have been copied from coracles introduced by <a href="/wiki/Prince_Madog" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Madog">Prince Madog</a> in the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mountbatten_class_hovercraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Mountbatten class hovercraft">Mountbatten class hovercraft</a> (MCH) entered commercial service in August 1968, initially between Dover and Boulogne but later also <a href="/wiki/Ramsgate" title="Ramsgate">Ramsgate</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pegwell_Bay" title="Pegwell Bay">Pegwell Bay</a>) to Calais. The journey time Dover to Boulogne was roughly 35&#160;minutes, with six trips per day at peak times. The fastest crossing of the English Channel by a commercial car-carrying hovercraft was 22&#160;minutes, recorded by the <i>Princess Anne</i> MCH SR-N4 Mk3 on 14 September 1995,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_air">By air</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: By air">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_Channel_crossings_by_air" title="List of English Channel crossings by air">List of English Channel crossings by air</a></div>\n<p>The first aircraft to cross the Channel was a <a href="/wiki/Balloon_(aircraft)" class="mw-redirect" title="Balloon (aircraft)">balloon</a> in 1785, piloted by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Blanchard" title="Jean-Pierre Blanchard">Jean Pierre Fran\xc3\xa7ois Blanchard</a> (France) and <a href="/wiki/John_Jeffries" title="John Jeffries">John Jeffries</a> (US).<sup id="cite_ref-EB_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Louis_Bl%C3%A9riot" title="Louis Bl\xc3\xa9riot">Louis Bl\xc3\xa9riot</a> (France) piloted the first airplane to cross in 1909.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_swimming">By swimming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: By swimming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_successful_English_Channel_swimmers" title="List of successful English Channel swimmers">List of successful English Channel swimmers</a></div>\n<p>The sport of Channel swimming traces its origins to the latter part of the 19th century when Captain <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Webb" title="Matthew Webb">Matthew Webb</a> made the first observed and unassisted swim across the Strait of Dover, swimming from England to France on 24\xe2\x80\x9325 August 1875 in 21&#160;hours 45&#160;minutes.\n</p><p>In 1927, at a time when fewer than ten swimmers (including the first woman, <a href="/wiki/Gertrude_Ederle" title="Gertrude Ederle">Gertrude Ederle</a> in 1926) had managed to emulate the feat and many dubious claims were being made, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Channel_Swimming_Association&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Channel Swimming Association (page does not exist)">Channel Swimming Association</a> (CSA) was founded to authenticate and ratify swimmers\' claims to have swum the Channel and to verify crossing times. The CSA was dissolved in 1999 and was succeeded by two separate organisations: <a href="/w/index.php?title=CSA_(Ltd)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="CSA (Ltd) (page does not exist)">CSA (Ltd)</a> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Channel_Swimming_and_Piloting_Federation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Channel Swimming and Piloting Federation (page does not exist)">Channel Swimming and Piloting Federation</a> (CSPF). Both observe and authenticate cross-Channel swims in the Strait of Dover. The Channel Crossing Association was set up at about this time to cater for unorthodox crossings.\n</p><p>The team with the most number of Channel swims to its credit is the <a href="/wiki/The_Serpentine" title="The Serpentine">Serpentine</a> Swimming Club in London,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> followed by the International <a href="/wiki/Sri_Chinmoy" title="Sri Chinmoy">Sri Chinmoy</a> Marathon Team.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of 2005, 811 people had completed 1,185 verified crossings under the rules of the CSA, the CSA (Ltd), the CSPF and Butlins.\n</p><p>The number of swims conducted under and ratified by the Channel Swimming Association to 2005 was 982 by 665 people. This includes 24 two-way crossings and three three-way crossings.\n</p><p>The number of ratified swims to 2004 was 948 by 675 people (456 men, 214 women). There have been 16 two-way crossings (9 by men and 7 by women). There have been three three-way crossings (2 by men and 1 by a woman). (It is unclear whether this last set of data is comprehensive or CSA only.)\n</p><p>The Strait of Dover is the busiest stretch of water in the world. It is governed by International Law as described in <i>Unorthodox Crossing of the Dover Strait Traffic Separation Scheme</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> It states: "[In] exceptional cases the French Maritime Authorities may grant authority for unorthodox craft to cross French territorial waters within the Traffic Separation Scheme when these craft set off from the British coast, on condition that the request for authorisation is sent to them with the opinion of the British Maritime Authorities."\n</p><p>The CCA, CSA, and CS&amp;PF are the organisations escorting channel swims, because their pilots have the experience, qualifications, and equipment to guarantee the safety of the swimmers they escort.\n</p><p>The fastest verified swim of the Channel was by the Australian <a href="/wiki/Trent_Grimsey" title="Trent Grimsey">Trent Grimsey</a> on 8 September 2012, in 6 hours 55 minutes,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> beating the previous record set in 2007 by Bulgarian swimmer <a href="/wiki/Petar_Stoychev" title="Petar Stoychev">Petar Stoychev</a>.\n</p><p>There may have been some unreported swims of the Channel, by people intent on entering Britain in circumvention of immigration controls. A failed attempt to cross the Channel by two Syrian refugees in October 2014 only came to light when their bodies were later discovered on the shores of the North Sea in Norway and the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_car">By car</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: By car">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 16 September 1965, two Amphicars crossed from Dover to Calais.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_types">Other types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Other types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="text-align:left">Date\n</th>\n<th style="text-align:left">Crossing\n</th>\n<th style="text-align:left">Participant(s)\n</th>\n<th style="text-align:left">Notes\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>27 March 1899\n</td>\n<td>First radio transmission across the Channel (from <a href="/wiki/Wimereux" title="Wimereux">Wimereux</a> to <a href="/wiki/South_Foreland_Lighthouse" title="South Foreland Lighthouse">South Foreland Lighthouse</a>)\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Guglielmo_Marconi" title="Guglielmo Marconi">Guglielmo Marconi</a> (Italy)\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
